Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This thesis presents an application of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model (FBIM) within
the Danish context, emphasizing the development and documentation of tactical intervention
strategies for fire emergency scenarios. Using the FBIM manual, dataset, and validation
interviews, the study focuses on the model’s practical implementation in Danish fire brigade
operations through two similar case studies.
The research defines the process of adapting FBIM to Danish realities, highlighting the
integration and adaptation of the model across different fire scenarios. Key goals include the
identification of critical adjustments necessary for aligning the model with Danish firefighting
protocols, highlighted by detailed case studies that showcase the model’s application in real-
world scenarios. The study documents the process, from the initial fire registration to the
deployment of control and extinguishment strategies, captured in detailed Gantt charts.
Results indicate that while FBIM provides a robust framework for tactical planning, certain
deviations from the manual require some modifications in order optimize its applicability in
Denmark. The empirical data, derived from local authorities and augmented by the Australasian
dataset, reveal nuances in firefighter procedures, requireing the need for localized adaptations.
The thesis concludes that FBIM’s integration into Danish fire safety design is not only feasible
but also beneficial, offering a structured approach to simulation of tactical firefighting. However,
it calls for continuous refinement and context-specific adjustments to harness the full potential
of the model, thereby enhancing the efficiency and effectiveness of fire brigade interventions in
Denmark.
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design

Chapter 1
Introduction
In both simple and complex structures, minimizing the risk of casualties and damage to the
building during the spread of fire is critical- To prevent the spread of fire and casualties,
there is a need for an update and optimization of fire intervention strategies in infrastructural
buildings. Especially the unconventional buildings, which due to their structural complexity
create unpredictable fire outcomes in no time and complex interventions.
The design process for buildings in Denmark follows the guidelines from Bygnings Reglerment
18 - Vejledninger (Building Regulations 18 - Guidelines) [1], which gives general guidelines, to
ensure the fire safety of both occupants and intervention crew. As the rules are general, it
creates situations, where the structure is not optimized for the needs of the fire brigade and
does not necessarily support a safe and effective intervention in case of fire.
As a result, there is a need to have a more optimal distribution of resources, to create a building
that is not expensive and at the same time friendly to fire brigades. Time is one of the most
important resources that are limited and play a key role in the rescue of people and structure.
To optimize the usage of this resource, and better understanding of the processes this master’s
thesis will investigate fire strategies and comparison of the Australian Fire Brigade Intervention
Model (FBIM) [2] in the Danish context. The model can convert the design of a building into
fire brigade intervention tasks, which can be quantified and can lead to the optimization of the
time and adapt the intervention strategies for the fire brigades.
This project aims to investigate the implementation of FBIM into Danish building regulations
and is done in two parts. The investigation of the usage of FBIM and investigation of the
dataset on which FBIM is founded. This project will focus on the first part which is described
below:
P. 1: The first part aims to investigate the usage of FBIM under Danish conditions to
conclude on the compatibility of FBIM. Thereby, a series of FBIM scenarios is developed
and the model is used to evaluate FBIM on danish fire intervention tactics reviewed by
intervention experts. The feedback will be used to develop the method of implementing
FBIM and illustrate the possibility of using the manual in danish realities.
The investigation of this Masters’s Thesis will be based on provided material from Danish
Technical University (DTU), Danish Emergency Management Agencies (DEMA), Airport Rescue
and Firefighting (ARFF), T˚arnby Municipality Fire Station (TMFS), which will create a solid
foundation for what is known for managing similar situations. By gaining knowledge in that
field, a model will be developed that offers theoretical tactics and strategies for the intervention
team.
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
1
==PAGE:10==
This will create a set of data, that will be validated by the authorized person who will provide
the feedback and guidelines, to evaluate the compatibility of FBIM and help in its continued
development. The investigation of this master’s thesis would provide the necessary data, that
will lead to a better understanding of what can be implemented, updated, or replaced in a
Danish fire system in unconventional buildings.
In addition, an investigation of FBIM usage will be done, using all of the steps provided in
the FBIM manual [2] through several scenarios that are developed in collaboration with DTU,
ARFF, TMFS. This method will provide a deeper understanding of the manual’s functionality
and the data that are required for its effective usage.
The successful application of this project has the potential to influence future designs of the
structures, therefore, the goal is to test and adapt this manual to Danish contexts.
As the numerous aspects that must be considered when working with creation of the tactics,
the reader is advised to work closely with the appendix. This will help establish the logic behind
the project’s methodologies that were used. It is important to use to the FBIM manual’s charts
(1-16) [2], datasets [3], flow diagrams, and to not forget to use all available support tools
provided within the document while exercising patience.

==PAGE:11==
Chapter 2
Theory
This chapter provides a fundamental understanding of key principles and aspects that lie behind
the creation of tactics related to firefighters’ interventions and extinguishing processes. It
will present the theoretical framework, aspects, and fundamental theories that help in the
development of realistic tactics for use in emergencies. The information provided is gathered
from various manuals and sources, creating a fundamental knowledge base for the creation of
theoretical tactics.
2.1
Fire and Rescue Service Organization
Figure 2.1: Danish Emergency Man-
agement Agency (DEMA)
The Danish Fire and Rescue Services operates at two
levels. Each of the levels, manages the tasks based
on the scale of emergencies. The responsibilities of
these organizations include responding to fires, natural
disasters, and significant incidents involving hazardous
chemicals, among other duties.
In Denmark, the task of ensuring public safety is managed at the municipal level, which is
divided into 32 municipalities. Each municipality is responsible for carrying out “everyday”
safety and emergency tasks within its borders. To improve their capabilities, municipalities
often cooperate to create cooperative municipal rescue services. These services operate strictly
within their respective municipalities and can be managed by several organizations, such as the
municipality’s fire and rescue services, the Danish Emergency Management Agency (DEMA)
(Beredskabsstyrelsen), private undertakings or voluntary organizations, which are particularly
common in southern Jutland.
DEMA operates on the national level, dividing Denmark into six regions, each of which it
administrates. The main objective is to assist local municipalities in the event of serious
emergencies that exceed the local capacity to manage. The support provided by the national
level includes augmenting personnel numbers if the mission escalates or exceeds the capacity
of existing forces. Additionally, the DEMA can supply specialized equipment that is typically
unavailable to local municipalities. The DEMA is stationed centrally in each of the six regions
and can be dispatched to any part of the region within an hour and to the country level within
two hours [4], [5].
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
3
==PAGE:12==
2.1.1
National Intervention Procedure
The documentation for the intervention procedures can be challenging to find and access.
The documentation and information are primarily drawn from interviews, the Danish Building
Code (BR 18), and operational manuals, including those on hose deployment and the roles
of technical leaders (officers in charge) that are published by DEMA. Furthermore, some of
the manuals are outdated, which creates a gap between theoretical knowledge and reality [6].
This gap led to interviews that provided updated knowledge, ending the development of an
intervention case that closely mirrors reality. The components mentioned will be described in
the following sections.
2.2
Regulation and intervention procedures
This chapter will supply important information regarding regulations and intervention procedures
related to fire safety design. This section underlines the importance of understanding and
applying the regulations and manuals for designing and operating buildings during emergencies.
Additionally, assumptions and structure of the fire brigades will be discussed below.
2.2.1
Fire Safety design: BR 18
Figure 2.2: Danish
Building Code 18
(BR18)
The project is centered on the application of the FBIM manual for iden-
tifying firefighters’ procedural tactics and quantifying them. Within these
tactics, BR 18 holds an important role since it directs designers in creating
structures that are safer and more helpful to emergency interventions.
The adoption of various safety measures slightly simplifies the intervention
tasks. These regulations are important not only for the structural design
but also for identifying fire locations and providing a fast evacuation of the
building before intervention begins. Once the model is created, designers
can verify whether the applied safety measures are adequate for ensuring
fast response from firefighters and if the structure is optimally designed for
emergency intervention. Furthermore, the practical experience can increase
future safety measures and contribute significantly to the development of
more effective building designs for the safety of occupants and firefighters.[1]
2.2.2
Establishment of hose connection
Figure 2.3: Establishment of
hose connection manual (Ind-
sats Slangeudlægning)
The hose coupling is the main activity that firefighters must
carry out at the beginning of almost any intervention. This leads
to ensuring and maintaining adequate water pressure for the
firefighters who are in the process of extinguishing the fire.
The manual “Indsats - Slangeudlægning” (Establishment of hose
connection) [Indsats] focuses on general operational procedures
that include the correct deployment of hoses, their maintenance
and operation.
Additionally, it describes the importance of
cooperation and the effectiveness of firefighters.
The manual provides information and usage of different types
of hoses, such as high pressure (also known as supplementary
hoses), B-, C-hoses, dry pipes, etc. This requires firefighters to have specialized knowledge and
form dedicated teams for this purpose. Overall, this information will contribute as a guideline
for the tactics and terminology of this project.
4
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:13==
In addition, the manual provides an overview of the different standard types of hose deployment,
allowing users to have an understanding of the process and the roles of each firefighter. On the
other hand, the manual also provides instructions, diagrams, and practical examples to show
each step of the process that firefighters have to go through.
2.2.3
Emergency Management
Figure 2.4: Emergency Man-
agement (Teknisk ledelse)
The technical management manual is divided into three parts,
which are principles of technical leadership, case studies, and an
explanation of concepts. The principles for technical manage-
ment are based on the guidelines for the management of cases
in emergencies, which is also known as REFIL (Retningslinjer
for indsatsledelse) (Guidelines for effort management) [7], along
with various management tools. The tools are designed to help
the technical leader (later denoted as the officer in charge)
conduct the emergency operation as optimally as possible. The
manual provides a guideline for how to handle large-scale emer-
gencies, which provides a detailed establishment of the command
post for more effective leadership of firefighters.
In addition to this, it illustrates two cases that show the usage of
the manual principles at emergency sites. The first case focuses
on more usual emergencies, e.g. fires in multi-story buildings.
This case tries to highlight the use of technical management
strategies like prioritization, relaying relevant orders, establishing
efficient logistics, and prioritizing in the evacuation of the building, which starts as The
second case is based on industrial emergencies, which involve hazardous materials, showcasing
specialized approaches on how to deal with such dangerous situations.
The main focus in this section is on the deployment of supporting management tools and the
setting up of the command post that will make conducting the emergency operation as optimal
as possible. These cases illustrate the flexibility of management tools in different situations.
2.2.4
Intervention on burning electrical and hybrid vehicles
Figure 2.5: Intervention on
burning electrical and hybrid
vehicles
Electrical vehicle (EV) technology has existed for several decades,
but its population has recently increased.
As a result, an
increasing number of fires involving EV’s is occurring.
For
now, this event is happening relatively rarely, but they pose a
significant challenge as there is no effective method to extinguish
the EV on the spot.
The current strategy focuses primarily on minimizing the risk
to people and structures by either moving the vehicle to a safer
place or allowing it to burn in a controlled area where it does
not affect the surroundings.
DEMA’s “Temahæfte indsats ved brand i el- og hybridbiler”
(Response to Fires in Electric and Hybrid Vehicles) [8] outlines
protocols and guidelines for handling fires in electric and hybrid
vehicles. Apart from this, the manual describes the safety pro-
cedures to ensure that the safety of firefighters is not exceeded
during the extinguishment process.
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
5
==PAGE:14==
The manual outlines two main strategies which are defensive
and offensive and the third one which is the combination of the two. The offensive strategy
allows the vehicle to burn in a controlled setting, while the offensive involves direct suppression
to cool the battery and extinguish the fire. Thirdly, a hybrid approach is used where the vehicle
can not burn in its current location. In such cases, firemen use offensive measures to control
the fire and cool the vehicle, and then move it to a safer place where it can burn safely
Additionally, the manual also describes a management procedure of how to handle a burning
electrical vehicle in the building as a parking garage or underground parking cellar. It includes
measures of ventilating smoke, assessing the risk of fire spreading and other measures to be
taken in this type of situation. The case that is mentioned in this manual is similar to the case
that will be investigated in this project and will be discussed later in this report.
2.2.5
Firefighters structure, work and education condition
The size of the team can be established based on the risk-based design and emergency situation
and may differ. The number of firefighters affects the time that it takes to complete the
intervention.
According to the regulations, specifically Chapter 2 in Bek nr 1085 dated
25/10/2019 [9] a firefighting operation must include at least one officer in charge (OIC) or a
technical leader (TL), and sufficient personnel to operate the equipment, allowing for flexibility
in the total number of crew members and the allocated equipment. It’s important to note that
the technical leader (Indsatsleder) and team leader (holdleder) will go under the same name
(OIC). Before the adoption of this legislation, the arrangement of a team was often defined as
1+3, 1+5, or 1+7, where “1” represents the OIC and the following numbers (3, 5, 7) indicate
the number of firefighters in the team.
At present, fire brigade structures, such as those mentioned above, are not required, but
many fire stations still maintain these structures. The most commonly used structure consists
of a six-person team (1 officer + 5 firefighters). In the case of small-scale emergencies, a
four-member team (1 officer + 3 firefighters) is often used, especially in medium-sized towns,
as this arrangement allows them to respond more quickly to incidents. If a brigade encounters
difficulties in the management of an emergency, additional support can be requested from other
municipal brigades or DEMA. Finally, the decision on the composition of the fire brigade lies
with the individual municipalities, which allows them to adapt the organization to their specific
needs.
It is necessary to recognize that all firefighters undergo uniform training, adapted to the range
of tasks they are expected to perform in the course of the rescue service’s training programs
however, to a large degree, be regarded as craftsmanship as experience is essential.
In addition, firefighters are required to take part in maintenance training. This training is
making sure that both professional and volunteer firefighters can maintain their skills in the
field of emergency response. The firefighters are required to go through 12 training sessions
throughout the each year, which last for two hours each. The training is based on the exercise
that is provided by the Danish Emergency Management Agency (DEMA) and municipalities’
emergency service, where 4 exercises is defined by DEMA and the rest by the individual
municipalities.
The organization of personnel in a fire station may vary, including not only the structure of the
firefighting team but also the types of employment. Firefighters can be divided into different
employment categories. One of the categories includes full-time firefighters who work at the
firehouse for 12 or 24 hours a day and work from one morning to the evening or morning the
next day. These firefighters are equipped to sleep and eat at the station. As soon as their
6
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:15==
shift ends, they receive a few days off from their firefighting duties, which they can use to
pursue secondary employment or other interests. For example, firefighters specializing in Airport
Rescue and Firefighting (ARFF) usually work every fourth day.
The second category relates to part-time employment, in particular for firefighters associated
with a specific fire station who often live or work nearby. Unlike full-time firefighters who may
be present during normal working hours (08-17), part-time firefighters have different schedules.
Each part-time firefighter is equipped with an alarm pager, which is activated when a fire alarm
goes off. Upon activation, they are required to quickly assemble at their designated fire station,
typically within a 5-minute window, before the brigade is dispatched to the emergency site.
This system ensures that a fire station can be active and fully staffed immediately in the event
of an emergency[10].
2.2.6
Deployed Fire Brigade resources
In view of the fact that no two fires or interventions are identical, it is necessary to define and
standardize several factors. Several factors that can be mentioned are the number of personnel,
the type of fire, the location of the fire, and the documentation of it. The standardization
of the factor allows the application and comparison of different tactics in the same case. In
this project, two different cases have been selected, which will be using two different tactics
under the same initial conditions. This methodology makes it possible to make a systematic
comparison of the effectiveness of different firefighting methods.
2.2.7
Assumptions
To investigate the usage of FBIM manual on each case, it is necessary to establish certain
assumptions to define the borders of the tactic. This is expected to prevent the complications
that can appear in the tactic, as they can grow large and complex. Most of the assumptions
will impact the distances and times that firefighters must travel between points A and B.
The assumptions are formulated based on interviews with firefighters about cases that were
created. The arrival of the engine and its position depend on how far the OIC has to go to the
fire central. Moreover, the engine is repositioned to an optimal position to create ideal access to
the hydrant and dry pipes as well as the installation of hoses from the hydrant to the engine and
from the engine to the dry pipe. All firefighters that are involved in this case are experienced
and have the practical knowledge necessary for this tasks. In addition, firefighters are organized
into Smoke Helmet Teams (SHT) numbered from 1 to 4, with each team consisting of two
members responsible for specific tasks.
In reality, fire engines are of different sizes and composition. In this case, the engines will
be equipped with a six-member team: one driver, two smoke helmet teams (SHTs), and one
officer in charge (OIC), which is called a 1 + 5 team. The main responsibility of the driver
is to ensure that the pumps are continuously providing adequate pressure for the firefighters
(and maintaining water levels). The OIC plays a crucial role, rushing towards the Fire Central,
supervising the firefighters, and acting like “eyes” for the Incident commander (TL). In addition,
an Incident commander (TL) arrives in a separate vehicle and heads straight to the fire central,
where the TL supervises and manages firefighting operations from this position.
The tasks performed by SHTs are reflected in the Fire Brigade Intervention Modeling (FBIM)
manual [2]. These tasks include the establishment of water connections by attaching hoses from
the hydrant to the engine and from the engine to the dry pipe, placing hoses from the dry pipe
tap to the fireplace, carrying out search and rescue missions, protecting the fire compartment,
controlling and extinguishing the fire, and managing the rotation of firefighters. The presence of
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
7
==PAGE:16==
CCTV in the given building implies that search and rescue is not the main focus of firefighters’
efforts, but it will still be the first task of the second SHT to arrive at the scene of the fire.
The firefighter must use a breathing apparatus (BA) due to the expectation of heavy smoke
emanating from the fireplace. This operation is limited to a period of 20 minutes, according
to DEMA’s manual “Temahæfte indsats ved brand I el-og hybridbiler” (Response to Fires in
Electric and Hybrid Vehicles) [8]. FBIM Dataset [11] will be used for quantifying the time data.
The decision to use FBIM data comes from the missing datasets. Those assumptions will be
applied in the creation of the tactics later in this project.
2.2.8
Australaisian Regulations & Interventions
Figure 2.6: Australian Fire and
Emergency Service Authorities
Council
The Australian regulations as in Denmark was created to
ensure the safety of people and the protection of property
and the environment from fires. These rules are based on
the performance regulatory system, which sets standards
for structures, fire safety measures, and procedures on how
different units have to react to emergencies. There are a
lot of regulations and rules for intervention, and the most
important ones will be listed below.
The Building Codes of Australia (BCA) form a part of the
National Construction Code (NCC), setting the minimum required standards for safety, including
fire safety, that designers must adhere to before construction can commence. This code provides
the regulations for material resistance capability, fire prevention methods, evacuation, etc. to
ensure the safety of the building and occupants.
The state regulations provide detailed guidelines for fire safety, including specific protocols
for evacuations, fire prevention, and the safety of industrial buildings but they vary between
different states. Along with this, the emergency services and interventions focus on prevention
strategies, post-fire investigations, etc., which differ from state to state. Those services follow
the rules that are individual to each state which adapts to ensure that fire safety during the
emergency will fit the specific needs of the state.
As a result, the Australasian Fire and Emergency Service Authorities Council (AFAC) created a
tool that implements the doctrine and regulations to ensure the safety of people across the
whole region. This tool is the Fire Brigade Intervention Modelling (FBIM)[2] which plays a
fundamental role in the creation of this thesis.
2.3
Fire Brigade Intervention Model - FBIM
The Australian Fire and Emergency Service Council (AFAC) has developed the Fire Brigade
Intervention Model (FBIM) [12] that functions as a procedural guideline to standardize and
increase the efficiency of the fire brigade operating in the performance-based regulation
framework of Australasia. The model aims to systematically capture the sequence of time-
critical tasks involved in fire brigade activities. The tasks that are including the notification,
dispatch, response, arrival, access, assessment, determination of strategy, establishment of
search and rescue operations, protection of exposures, environmental protection, and finally,
control and extinguishing of the fire.
The FBIM manual [12] is developed based on literature reviews, doctrines, statistical analy-
sis, measurement analysis, and exercise practices with a focus on real values of firefighters’
performance and probabilities. The manual contains 16 charts that show the activities that
8
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:17==
the firefighters will carry out in a real fire situation. They are carefully designed to meet
the performance requirements that are laid down by building standards and international fire
engineers’ guidelines.
2.3.1
Applications
Fire Brigade Intervention Modeling (FBIM) [2] is a tool that can complete relevant building
codes and fire engineering guidelines designed with a range of activities, that the fire and fire
brigades go through during an intervention.
The methodology set out in the manual is intended to calculate the total time required to carry
out an intervention. A simplified version of the software has been developed to facilitate the
use of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model (FBIM) model in real-world settings. This software
integrates the manual and dataset, allowing faster simulations of the intervention.
2.3.2
Limitations
Figure 2.7: The operators
used in FBIM
The FBIM manual is not designed to predict the spread of fire
or smoke. For that purpose, some programs that are based on
computational fluid dynamics (CFD) or similar technologies are
provided for such simulations.
Despite this, there has been a
development of software that, although initially based on the
FBIM manual. The original software is not available for this study,
so a simplified version of the software was created.
2.3.3
FBIM Chart and Dataset:
At this step, there will be a merging of the manual and dataset
to gain a better understanding of how the two function together.
Before reaching into the types of charts that are available, it is
necessary to provide a fundamental explanation.
In the flowchart, the rhombus symbol marks a critical decision
point necessary for progressing through the diagram. The grey
box with a letter in the top right corner directs the firefighter or
engineer to a specific table in the dataset for guidance. Conversely,
the grey box without a letter signifies an action that must be taken,
often requiring calculation or estimation, in response to situations
encountered during fire intervention. The dark grey box signifies
a need for sub-charts, which provide additional actions necessary
to complete the chart in which the user is engaged. Red shaded
boxes highlight potential risks, including the possibility of loss of
life or other undesirable outcomes.

systematic method for creating a
quantified time estimate from when

the information is fully transferred
to the brigade notification. To get

is necessary to use the dataset.

Chart 4: Time for firefighters to respond to dispatch call
Figure 2.15: Chart 4
Chart 4 focuses on calculating the time it takes for the

fire engine receives the information.
The main purpose of the diagram is to create a realistic
time estimation to see how long time it will take for a fire
engine to reach the kerb side from the time that the fire
engine received the information.
To fully extract information from this flowchart, it is nec-

Figure 2.16: Table F

tions the first relates the fire en-
gine’s travel time to the site, and
the second deals with the dura-
tion of the fire engine moving
through the site.
This section
of the table requires various in-
put data, such as the address of
the building/fire site, the loca-

or the position of the responding
firefighters, the distance between
these two points, and the travel
time under different conditions
(for example, during morning or evening rush hours, on various days of the week).
After filling in all the necessary details in the table, an estimated time interval is gained,
indicating how long it will take for the fire engine to arrive at the kurb side. Other than, the
time estimation is done by the geo position system “Google Maps” providing a conservative
time travel estimation.

Chart 9: Time for firefighter travel
Figure 2.36: Chart 9
Chart 9 is a strategic tool that is
used to calculate the time firefight-

scene. The flowchart directs users
on the types of movements that fire-
fighters must make to reach key

A few aspects are important to con-
sider when using this chart. Firstly,
it is necessary to determine whether
doors need to be negotiated. Sec-
ondly, the times for horizontal and
vertical travel must be determined.
Horizontal travel time is calculated
by estimating the distance between
two main points and using the travel
speed to establish the time of hori-
zontal movement. In addition, there
is a vertical travel speed, which can
be achieved through stairs, fire el-
evators, or similar means. Vertical
travel time is established by estimat-
ing the type of vertical movement
(for example, elevators or stairs) and
using the table of travel speeds to
calculate the time required for verti-
cal movement.
The main purpose of this flowchart is to create an understanding of what and how long distance
the firefighters must travel. Furthermore, the chart seeks to provide the total time a firefighter
takes to reach various points at the fire scene. To obtain information from the charts, it is
also necessary to use the dataset. In this context, Tables I, J, Q, R, and T were used. It is
important to note that statistical data support all these tables.

firefighter to breach through various types of opening barriers (e.g., roller security doors, inward
opening, side hung doors). See table in figure 2.20.
Table J describes the time it takes a firefighter to enter a door that is locked, using keys for
access. See the table in figure 2.21

firefighters’ speed with varying levels of equipment load, which can be seen in the Table of
Figure 2.30.
Table R describes the vertical travel speed using a fireman’s lift and the loading of equipment
in the lift and can be seen in the Table of Figure 2.31.
Table T provides information on vertical speed when using stairs, including data on firefighters’
speed with different equipment loads, which can be seen in table 2.32.

Chart 12: Time for search and rescue
Figure 2.42: Chart 12
Chart 12 is designed to make sure
that the safety of the personnel is
not exceeded and that they can con-
duct search and rescue operations.
If the safety level is kept on adequate
level, the flowchart provides tactics
for executing a search and rescue
operation in the fire compartment.
There are two types of search mis-
sions, which are primary and sec-
ondary. The primary search is con-
ducted on the exposed area/floor,
where the firefighter walks through
the area, checking all corners and
rooms within the fire compartment
floor area with a thermal camera.
In contrast, the secondary search
involves firefighters crawling into ar-
eas with low visibility to ensure that
all occupants have been evacuated
from the fire-exposed area. Addi-
tionally, the chart includes a step for
searching internal places of safety
(save rooms) within the building.
This step is prioritized if the building
has such rooms within its structure.
Both searches consider the area and distances so that the time can be calculated.
The main goal of the flowchart is to ensure that search and rescue operations are conducted
effectively and safely by managing the risk to firefighters inside the fire compartment. After
establishing the tactic, it is necessary to sum up all the steps, which will provide the total time
needed to complete the search and rescue operation.
To gain the necessary information from the charts, it is necessary to use the dataset. In this
case, Tables Q, R, S, T, and Y were used. It is crucial to acknowledge that the statistical
data support all the tables.

on firefighters’ speeds with different equipment loads. The table is displayed in Figure 2.30
Table R describes the vertical travel speed employing a fireman’s lift, along with the equipment
load in the lift. The table is displayed in Figure 2.31
Table S describes the hindrance factor caused by evacuating occupants, which can be calculated
through simulations or analogous methods. The table can be seen in Figure 2.34
Table T describes vertical speeds when using stairs, presenting diverse data on firefighters’
speeds with different equipment loads, particularly under light smoke conditions. The table can
be seen in 2.32

fering additional support to prevent
the fire from spreading to nearby
properties. Additionally, the chart
helps to restructure the strategy.
The main purpose of the flowchart is to protect the properties that are surrounding the fire and
not exposed it to, aiming to minimize the damage from fire or water. Additionally, there is a
need to sum up all the steps to calculate the total time it takes to protect uninvolved content.
In this chart, no table dataset is used. The time and items are determined by using user input
in steps 2, 3, and 7, in the form of a qualified guess, which leads to a significant deviation in
the end-time result.
2.3.3.15
Chart 15: Time to control and extinguish the fire
Chart 15 is designed to establish the time it takes to control and extinguish a fire. The
flowchart provides the sequence of events that evaluate the cooling effect of the applied water
on the fire’s heat release rate (HRR).
Three outcomes are possible when water is applied. If the cooling effect is lower than the
HRR of the fire, then the water does not affect either controlling or extinguishing the fire and
necessitates a backup plan. If the cooling effect is equal to the HRR, the fire is considered under
control, and no further spread of the fire is expected, but it is still not enough to extinguish
24
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:33==
the fire. Lastly, if the cooling effect is greater than the HRR of the fire, then the fire may be in
the process of being extinguished.
Figure 2.47: Chart 15
After identifying the type of fire, it
is possible to estimate how long it
will take to extinguish the fire. If
the fire cannot be extinguished, the
flowchart returns to the beginning
of sub chart 11 to establish the
necessary water supply, redoing the
process.
The main purpose of the flowchart
is to increase the effectiveness of
controlling and extinguishing the fire.
If the Heat Release Rate (HRR) is
not decreasing, it is recommended
to call for backup which can provide
the necessary tools and equipment.
This chart represents the most im-
portant step in the strategy for extin-
guishing fires. Once the tactic has
been established, it is necessary to
summarize all the steps, which will
then provide the total time required
to control and extinguish the fire.
In this chart, no dataset in the form of a table is used. Instead, the time and items are determined
by using user input in step 6, either in the form of a qualified guess or through calculations,
for example, in Computational Fluid Dynamics simulations (CFD) or other simulation tools.
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
25
==PAGE:34==
2.3.3.16
Chart 16: Time to protect the environment
Figure 2.48: Chart 16
Chart 16 is designed to analyze and
reduce the effect of the firefighting
process on the environment. The
process begins by identifying what
is burning and what is the environ-
mental impact, particularly the ef-
fects of wastewater and the toxicity
of smoke. The flowchart provides
a set of questions that users must
answer while establishing a tactic. If
the resources that are already pro-
vided at the site are insufficient, the
chart offers options to increase the
water supply or to call for backup
through the use of sub charts.
The main purpose of this flowchart
is to account for the environmental
impact and create conditions that
will minimize the environmental im-
pact of fire and its control through
extinguishment. This includes con-
sidering the effects of the fire on the
atmosphere and water. If there is
a risk of significant environmental
impact, it is recommended to call
for additional resources to prevent
the spread of materials that could
harm the environment. Additionally,
it is necessary to tally all the steps,
which will provide the total time required to complete the protection of the environment.
In this chart, no table dataset is used. The gathering of time and items relies on user input
from step 2, either through a qualified guess or calculations, for instance, in CFD or other
simulation tools.
2.3.3.17
Summary
In conclusion, charts can be repeatedly used depending on what type of events need to be
completed. Depending on the specific case, it is also possible to skip the charts. In general,
the sequence of charts is very case-specific.
The primary goal is to establish a logical progression and conclude all necessary activities using
charts. In cases of ”overly generalized steps,” it is possible to introduce new charts or steps.
This aspect will be explored further in the report. However, it is important to recognize that
the charts cover the majority of actions that firefighters must go through. Any new chart or
step must receive validation from authorities before the modifications can be implemented in
the provided charts. For a more detailed explanation of tables and charts, refer to the FBIM
Dataset and Manual A.1, [2], [3].

Chapter 3

3.1
Development and Usage of software
Figure 3.1: Chart operators
As simulations are time consuming, it is necessary to create a
method that is both optimal and quick for running simulations.
The FBIM manual and dataset can be enhanced by integrating
them into a single system (software). This optimization approach
will be discussed in this section.[2],[11]
The software was developed on the basis of the FBIM manual
and primarily shows charts without complex calculations related
to fire development or similar processes. It contains a simple
flowchart that allows users to quickly navigate through the charts
without having to flip through the manuals but after users get
familiar with it. The main purpose of this process is to make
the search and application of the charts more efficient.
The Python programming language was used in the development
of the software. Python is a well-known programming language
for solving algorithmic problems as well as an open resource.
In this study, Python was chosen because of the simplicity of
the coding. This software was used because of the user-friendly
learning process, which allowed a faster understanding of the
main rules of the software.[13]
The FBIM charts follow a basic structure that provides a question
requiring a “YES” or “NO” answer from the user. Once the
question has been answered, the user is directed to one of the
six boxes shown in Figure ??. The user must then continue
in accordance with the chart’s instructions based on six action
process boxes A.1, A.3 The user decision is represented by a
rhombus shape while the user input box is depicted as a grey
box without a letter in the top right corner and the table display
box is depicted as a grey box with a letter in the top right corner.
The sub-chart is indicated with dark gray box, and the danger
box are shown as a red square. The software employs “IF,”
“ELIF,” and “ELSE” statements to follow a logical route through
these boxes.

requiring the use of tables, sub-charts, or other question. The software automatically imports
the necessary charts or relevant tables. When a user enters a box in which users must specify
the relevant materials and their associated times, he or she is asked to enter the name of the
item or action and its respective time. The necessary information will be collected from the
case where the intervention is taking place.

location” (T20).

3.2
Model Formulation & Data Gathering
This section provides the methodology that was used in the creation and usage of the Fire
Brigade Intervention Model (FBIM). It describes the iterative process of setting up and using
the model, as well as the formulation of realistic fire scenarios for the application of FBIM. The
section includes the importance of pre-modeling assessments and the dynamic nature of FBIM
in responding to fire situations. Overall, Section 3 provides insights into the approach taken to
simulate and analyze fire scenarios within the study.
3.2.1
Pre-Modeling Assessment
To be able to use the FBIM manual there is a need to create of hypothetical but realistic fire
scenario. This model sums up all the main activities and steps to approximate a real-case
scenario. However, it is important to recognize that while the model is “large”, it is not without
its imperfections and requires adjustments to reach its realism. The FBIM’s dynamic nature
in responding to fire situations can play an important role in both firefighters’ and engineers’
design of structures and tactics.
The upcoming cases will illustrate the types of data and information necessary for the FBIM to
construct a scenario that mirrors reality as closely as possible and to develop the methodology of
use and documentation. To effectively translate such a complex subject as brigade intervention
into actionable strategies, the engineers and fire brigades must address several issues, which
will be delineated in the sections that follow.
3.2.1.1
Structural scenario formulation:
The initial step involves defining a theoretical fire scenario in a location where the execution
of fire extinguishing might cause challenges. Such scenarios could include fires at the core
of a building, on hospital top floors, or within prisons as the use of FBIM is primarily aimed
at non-conventional fire scenarios/buildings. By applying the model to these hypothetical
scenarios, potential bottlenecks and elements that may consume precious time will become
evident.
To confirm the scenario is as realistic as possible and can be used in the Danish context, it is
necessary to investigate the Danish Fire Safety System and its protocols to gather the necessary
information about tools and equipment that are used by firefighters. This investigation will
provide a clearer understanding of the variety of equipment and manpower required to conduct
this scenario from a Danish point of view.
Collaboration with Fire Brigade Officers is very important. This phase serves as a measure of
the deviation between theoretical knowledge and practical experience. This phase will provide
the information that will help in formulating a structured and realistic strategy and case. It is
important to note that the strategy is not universally applicable which leads to the necessity of
adjustments to the theoretical model to the specific cases.
Furthermore, another significant factor that needs to be considered, is the concept of time
milestones. This concept is crucial because it determines the arrival, start, and completion
times of various tasks, in addition to setting time limits for firefighters equipped with breathing
apparatus (BA) operating within fire compartments. This is a critical instrument that must
be considered carefully in the formulation and refinement of strategies. Besides that time
milestones also make any comparison to real incidents more readily along with transparency
when evaluating the results
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
31
==PAGE:40==
After combining all the above-stated information into a model, a discussion with authorities is
required.
Note: It is important to understand that not all charts are applicable to every scenario.
Therefore, it is necessary to avoid the inclusion of charts merely because the model provides 16
charts.
3.2.2
Interviews
The connection between practical and theoretical knowledge is mainly established through
interviews with the Airport Rescue and Fire Fighting (ARFF) department. These discussions
not only build a connection between theory and practice, but also link the knowledge gaps left
by the literature that could not provide it. Since the tactics are dynamic and highly situational,
it is challenging to keep them in a fixed framework applicable to every potential incident.
Introducing the FBIM manual to firefighters caught their interest and willingness to assist by
providing all the necessary information that was missing to complete the cases based on FBIM.
To adapt these models for real-world usage, many interviews were conducted. The goal was
to explain different tactical details to someone with no experience in firefighter tactics and
settle on the scenario descriptions. The process began with defining a generic scenario with a
specific fire development inside a fire compartment. Afterward, these scenarios were iteratively
reviewed and evaluated with key insights from the firefighters’ experiences, thereby achieving a
more accurate reflection of reality.
Figure 3.8: The diagram over iterative
process
The iterative process that is shown in the Figure 3.8,
including the feedback received during the meetings,
are targeted to adjust and evaluate the data selected
from the FBIM manual and Dataset. It also aims to
provide a realistic time estimation for the case duration.
(updating or termination).
This cycle of feedback and adaptation served, not only
to evaluate the model but also to identify parts of the
FBIM model that might have been too optimistic. To
illustrate some of these processes, the Figures below
visualize the methodology behind the iterative process.
The Figures 3.9a and 3.9b illustrate a segment of Case 1 P6-8. The black text outlines the
initial case description, presenting the concept and the imagined processes behind the fire
emergency in cellar P6-8. This is a basic case depiction. Meanwhile, the red text summarizes
the internal discussion, verification, and estimation by a person with experience in firefighting
methods. Such a discussion develops the logical sequence of events by integrating or excluding
elements or actions that firefighters are likely or unlikely to undertake. Lastly, the green text
represents feedback and observations from firefighters, serving as the final step in confirming the
case description. This phase explains the actions firefighters would undertake when confronting
a fire in the specific locations and under the defined circumstances.

(a) The internal discussion of the case
(b) External validation of the case

After creating all the necessary adjustments based on the interview comments and implementing
the FBIM model, the stage of tactic validation begins. This validation process is shown by a

of validating the tactics that have been developed. The Figure below shows one of the slides,

required to complete the discussed task. The outcomes were observed and afterward compared.
3.2.3
Measurements
This part of this study is to use measurement tools for the quantification of potential routes
that will be taken by firefighters during the fire emergency within the environmental conditions.
This task incorporates the process of creating a systematic list that will make the process simple
and comprehensive.
For a systematic accumulation of data, it is advisable to create sketches of the floor levels
and perspective drawings of the staircases to gain a visual understanding of the structure’s
arrangement (if floor plans are not available).
Doing so will simplify and rationalize the
geographical placement of measurements. However, if the measurements are inaccurate or
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
33
==PAGE:42==
incorrect, it is important to not continue working with this data, as the model highly depends
on precise measurements.
Below is a guideline that can help create a proper plan for the data collection:
Type of Data
Distances between the entrance and exiting rooms in the cellar.
Counting the steps and measuring landing lengths.
The status of the doors and their amounts.
Expected visibility level in the given fire compartment.
Assumption regarding occupancy.
Estimate hidden factors, such as occupancy, how full is the parking cellar, etc.
Type of sketches
Create a floor plan sketch.
Create a perspective drawing of complicated figures (such as stairs).
of course, there are many more things that are mentioned above. but the main idea is to
establish a list of things that is necessary to register.
3.2.4
Preliminary Model Formulation
Given that tactics can vary significantly depending on the fire scene and how it is read by the
commander, it is necessary to create a sketch that will describe the general strategy for the fire
brigade intervention. For someone not specialized in fire engineering, crafting an accurate and
adequate flowchart right from the start can be challenging, which is to be expected. However,
the question arises: where should one begin? This section of the report will explore and offer
several case examples on this topic.
At the start, the user must question themselves about the types of events that need to be
managed throughout the process. This includes the emergence of the fire: why did the fire
occur? How did the ignition start? Where is the fire located? The registration process: How
was the fire detected? Which system is responsible for alerting the dispatch center? The arrival
of the Fire Brigade(s): where do they arrive? How many engines are present? What kinds of
engines are arriving? The extinguishing process: how many hoses are needed? Is the area filled
with smoke? What is the likelihood of the fire spreading to neighboring items? Search and
rescue operations: are there individuals inside the building? And lastly, exterior conditions: how
does the structure contribute to suppressing the fire? Is there a sprinkler system? Are there
fire doors? etc.
The questions outlined above aim to create an overall understanding of the event flow, the
fire’s origin, detection, registration, notification to the alarm center, arrival and preparation of
firefighters, rescue of individuals inside the building, and the fire’s extinguishment.
Of course, there may be many additional questions, but the main idea is to form an overall
understanding of the events within the case that will be investigated from beginning to end.
Afterward, it’s necessary to adapt the case by adding or excluding information or tasks that are
not needed in the current situation.
34
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:43==
For example, within the context of a search and rescue operation, the building that will be
investigated contains a closed-circuit television (CCTV) system. Such a system is capable of
verifying that there are no individuals on a given floor leading to a reduction of the search and
rescue mission from an entire floor to specifically the area where the fire started. It is important
to avoid overcomplication of the procedure at this stage. If the user does so it might result in
a scenario where the user is unable to complete the main logical sequence, and fail to reflect a
realistic intervention. Once the logic is established, the user is then required to implement the
fire brigade intervention modeling (FBIM) manual, but at a general level.
For the moment, it is important to determine the kinds of charts necessary to create a general
but logical path and to modify the method according to the possibilities in the FBIM manual.
Note: The following flowcharts in their current state is not the final version. The flowcharts
will progressively be updated into more “well thought” versions. This process is dependent on
additional calculations and latent events, that become visible only when the user fully submerses
in the process and milestone time estimates are obtained.
3.2.4.1
Case 1 Scenario 1
In this case, the fire brigade arrives in two engines: The first, known as the Fire Engine
(Autosprøjte), is equipped with two smoke helmet teams (SHT), which contain firefighters #1
to #4, an officer in charge (OIC), and a driver (D). The second, a standard vehicle, houses
a technical intervention leader (Indsatsleder ISL) and a driver. But for the purposes of this
scenario, it will referred to as the technical leader engine (TLE). The structure of brigades:
• Fire Engine 1 (Response 1) contains 2 SHT (#1 &#2), 1 OIC, and 1 D. (T˚arnby
Municipality Fire Station).
• Fire Engine 2 (Response 2) contains 2 SHT (#3 & #4), 1 OIC and 1 D. (Airport Fire
Station West).
• Technical Fire Leader Engine (Response 1) contains TL and 1 D. (T˚arnby Municipality
Fire Station).
This information will later be useful in the detailed accounting of the firefighters’ actions. After
defining the necessary number of brigades required for intervention, the general logic path can
be described.
The Deployment Process:
Intervention occurs after the fire information is received and
confirmed, as shown in Chart 1, which lays the foundation for the intervention strategy.
Afterward, the information is processed and the fire engines are dispatched to the fire scene
(Charts 2 – 4).
Coordination of Intervention:
After arrival, the Officer in Charge (OIC) and/or Technical
Leader (TL) rushes towards the fire central to gain a complete overview of the fire’s detection,
and directs the engines to their respective positions within this fireside, as indicated in Chart 5.
Besides that, the TL/OIC will determine the necessity for backup and its specific type (Chart
7).
Tactical Implementation:
The process that involves reaching the setup areas, accessing
water supplies, and approaching the fire area, is illustrated in Charts 8, 10, and 11. It involves
the horizontal and vertical traveling of SHT #1 to #4, and the deployment of hoses connected
to hydrants, engines, and dry pipes.
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
35
==PAGE:44==
Search and Rescue Operation:
SHT #2 to #4 are tasked with searching for people within
the fire compartment and assessing how heat radiation impacts the structure’s exterior (Charts
12 and 13).
Control and Extinguish the Fire:
SHT #1 and #3 will focus on controlling and ultimately
extinguishing the fire, as detailed in Chart 15. This structure describes a sequential and logical
approach, providing vision into the processes the fire brigade must undertake to effectively
extinguish a fire.

3.2.4.2
Case 1 Scenario 2
Scenario 2 requires the same process for both of the second scenarios in cases 1 and 2. The
strategy involves one brigade that arrives at the fire scene and a second brigade being requested
as backup by the officer in charge or technical leader (OIC/TL). Let’s look closer at the
resources that this scenario has.
The respond 1 (R1) dispatch includes the Fire Engine (Autosprøjte) and the Technical Leader
Engine (TLE). The Fire Engine (FE) contains two smoke helmet teams (SHT) which contain
firefighters #1 to #4, an officer in charge (OIC), and a driver (D). The technical leader
(Indsatsleder) arrives in a distinct vehicle (TLE), which includes a driver and technical leader.
In this scenario, the OIC will initiate a call for backup, details of which are also included here.
The structure of the brigades:
• Fire Engine 1 (Response 1) contains 2 SHT (#1 & #2), 1 OIC, and 1 D. (T˚arnby
Municipality Fire Station).
• Technical Fire Leader Engine (Response 1) contains TL and 1 D. (T˚arnby Municipality
Fire Station).
• Fire Engine 2 (Response 2) contains 2 SHT (3 4), 1 OIC and 1 D. (Store Magleby Fire
Station)
The Deployment Process:
As in scenario 1, the process begins with receiving and verifying
the fire alert (Chart 1), which then starts the intervention process. This initial information lays
the foundation for defining which tactics will be used. Afterward, the information is processed,
and the fire engine is dispatched to the fire scene (Charts 2 – 4).
Coordination of the Event:
Once the brigade reaches the fire scene, an officer in charge
(OIC) and/or a technical leader (TL) will quickly head toward the fire central to evaluate the
situation and locate the fire’s location (Chart 5). Afterward, from the fire central, the OIC/TL
will request additional support by calling for another fire brigade identical to the first (Chart 7).
Tactical Implementation:
After the OIC/TL finds the fire’s location, they will relay the
orders to reposition the fire engine to a specific area within the fire scene. After Fire Engine’s
(FE) arrival, the Smoke Helmet Team (SHT) will start the preparation steps for extinguishing
the fire, which include search and rescue operations and securing a water source (Charts 8, 10,
11). This phase also covers the coordination of firefighters’ movements, both horizontally and
vertically.
Search and Rescue Operation:
The search and rescue mission will be initiated by SHT
#1 following SHT #2s successful establishment of a water supply. Once the water supply
is in place, SHT #2 will begin their mission focused on exposure protection. This phase is
important for detecting people in need of evacuation and areas requiring extra measures to
stop the fire or smoke spread (Charts 12 and 13).
Control and Extinguish the Fire:
The Smoke Helmet Team #1 (SHT) will start the fire
control process. Once the SHT #2 has completed its initial task, it will assist SHT #1, and
together they will begin extinguishing the fire (Chart 15). At this stage, Response 2, serving as
backup, will be prepared to substitute the SHTs operating within the fire compartment.
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
37
==PAGE:46==
Figure 3.12: ”Guess” of a process in case 1 scenario 2
Backup:
The Team Leader/Officer in Charge (TL/OIC) will request backup. The arrival of
Response 2 follows the same path as Response 1, including information gathering, confirmation,
38
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:47==
and the deployment of the engine towards the fire scene (Charts 2-4). As the key tasks such as
establishing water connections and laying hoses have already been established by Response 1,
the primary role of Response 2, is to provide support to Response 1 when needed.
It is important to recognize that the case descriptions work as guidelines for users, which will
help in the creation of more precise scenarios that will be used later in the report. This draft of
the process creates a flow that can give an idea of what processes firefighters have to complete
to extinguish the fire.
3.2.4.3
Case 2 Scenario 1
Case 2 is happening on the 6th floor of the parking building, where an electrical vehicle starts
a fire, which triggers the sprinkler system. The area is open and exposed, allowing a steady
stream of fresh air through the parking structure. From a strategic viewpoint, Case 2 Scenario
1 mirrors in many ways the Case 1 Scenario 1, making them pretty similar. The tactic is based
on the immediate notification of fire brigades (Response 1 and 2). After their arrival, the officer
in charge (OIC/TL) rushes to the fire central and, if necessary, requests additional forces. The
first response, containing the Fire Engine (Autosprøjte), which is equipped with two smoke
helmet teams (SHT), which contain firefighters #1 to #4, an officer in charge (OIC), and a
driver (D). The second, a standard vehicle, houses a technical intervention leader (Indsatsleder
ISL) and a driver. But for the purposes of this scenario, it will referred to as the technical
leader engine (TLE) and finally, a Water Tender (vandtankv˚agn), manned only by a driver
(D). The secondary response features a single fire engine staffed with two smoke helmet teams
(SHT), one officer in charge (OIC), and a driver (D). The structure of the brigades:
• Fire Engine 1 (Response 1) contains 2 SHT (#1 & #2), 1 OIC, and 1 D. (T˚arnby
Municipality Fire Station).
• Technical Fire Leader Engine (Response 1) contains TL and 1 D. (T˚arnby Municipality
Fire Station).
• Water tender (Response 1) contains 1 D. (T˚arnby Municipality Fire Station)
• Fire Engine 2 (Response 2) contains 2 SHT (3 4), 1 OIC and 1 D. (Airport Fire Station
West)
The following section describes the general strategy to be used to handle this emergency.
The Deployment Process:
Intervention occurs after the fire information is received and
confirmed, as shown in Chart 1, which lays the foundation for the intervention strategy.
Afterward, the information is processed, and the fire engines are sent to the fire scene (Charts
2 – 4).
Coordination of Event:
After arrival, the Officer in Charge (OIC) and/or the Tactical Leader
(TL) immediately rushes toward the fire central to gather more information regarding the fire
location and the route firefighters must take to access the fire area. With this information at
hand, the OIC/TL can reposition the engines and gear up for the intervention.
Tactical Implementation:
The strategy for reaching the setup area, establishing a water
supply with adequate pressure, and entering the fire compartment is described in subsequent
charts (Charts 8-11). This involves both vertical and horizontal movements, the connection of
hoses to supply engines and dry pipes.
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
39
==PAGE:48==
Figure 3.13: ”Guess” of a process in case 2 scenario 1
Search and Rescue Operation:
Smoke Helmet Teams (SHTs) will conduct searches and
rescue for people inside the fire compartment and evaluate how heat radiation impacts the
areas outside of the fire compartment (Charts 12 and 13).
Control and Extinguish the Fire:
After the establishment of a water supply, SHTs will focus
on controlling and extinguishing the burning vehicle inside a fire compartment (Chart 15).
The usage of the charts follows a logical sequence, which is established based on the Danish
40
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:49==
Emergency Management Agency (DEMA) and interviews [Intreview/Need some proof, the
firefighters follow a plan, so we have to use that plan here] This type of approach creates clarity
and consistency in the execution of tasks from registration of the fire to its extinguishment. It
is important to understand that the case descriptions serve as guidelines for users. These are
fundamental for creating more detailed scenario descriptions, that will be investigated in results
later in the report.
3.2.4.4
Case 2 Scenario 2
Scenario 2 requires a similar approach as the previous scenario. The strategy starts with
the notification of a fire station and to dispatch of a unit to the scene of the fire. After an
investigation of the fire scene by the Officer in Charge/Technical Leader (OIC/TL), the backup
will be called for. Now a closer investigation will be performed to identify the types of resources
given for both response 1 and 2 to handle this type of emergency. The Response 1 is equipped
with three engines: a Fire Engine, which is staffed with two smoke helmet teams (SHT), an
officer in charge (OIC), and a driver (D); a Technical Leader Vehicle, staffed by a technical
leader (TL) and a driver (D); and finally, a water tender (vandtankv˚agn), operated solely by
a driver (D). The backup team consists of a single fire engine, which carries one OIC, two
SHTs, and a driver (D). A general description of the tactics to be employed in managing this
emergency will follow below.
• Fire Engine 1 (Response 1) contains 2 SHT (#1 & #2), 1 OIC, and 1 D. (T˚arnby
Municipality Fire Station).
• Technical Fire Leader Engine (Response 1) contains TL and 1 D. (T˚arnby Municipality
Fire Station).
• Water tender (Response 1) contains 1 D. (T˚arnby Municipality Fire Station)
• Fire Engine 2 (Response 2) contains 2 SHT (#3 & #4), 1 OIC and 1 D. (Store Magleby
Fire Station)
The Deployment Process:
As in scenario 1, the process begins with receiving and verifying
the fire alert (Chart 1), which then starts the intervention process. This initial information lays
the foundation for defining which tactics will be used and how. Afterward, the information is
processed, and the fire engine is sent to the fire scene (Charts 2 – 4).
Coordination of the Event:
Once the brigade reaches the fire scene, an officer in charge
(OIC) and/or a technical leader (TL) will quickly head toward the fire central to evaluate the
situation and locate the fire’s location (Chart 5). Afterward, from the fire central, the OIC/TL
will request additional support by calling for another fire brigade identical to the first (Chart 7).
Tactical Implementation:
To guarantee a safe and optimal environment for the firefighting
team, it’s fundamental to secure a water source, establish a set up area, and safely approach
the fire compartment (Charts 8-11). This phase also contains the horizontal and vertical
movements of the firefighters, connecting hoses from the water source to the engine, and
connecting to dry pipes.
Search and Rescue Operation:
Should there be individuals within the fire area, Search and
Rescue Teams (SHTs) will undertake the mission to find and save them. Additionally, assessing
the fire’s impact on the building’s structure is feasible if necessary (Charts 12-13).
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
41
==PAGE:50==
Figure 3.14: ”Guess” of a process in case 2 scenario 2
42
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:51==
Control and Extinguish the Fire:
After the establishment of a water supply and search and
rescue, firefighters will focus on containing and extinguishing the fire (Chart 15), continuing
their mission until additional support arrives that can substitute them.
Backup:
The Team Leader/Officer in Charge (TL/OIC) will request backup. The arrival of
Response 2 follows the same path as Response 1, including information gathering, confirmation,
and the deployment of the engine towards the fire scene (Charts 2-4). The most critical parts
of the fire scene have been taken under control, the primary goal of response 2 is to support
response 1 by establishing a water supply to the water tender, substituting for response 1 (Chart
8-11), or waiting for further instructions from the TL/OIC. Again, it is important to understand
that the case descriptions serve as guidelines for users. These are fundamental for creating
more detailed scenario descriptions, that will be investigated in results later in the report.
3.2.4.5
Final Model Formulation
The creation of the sketch is only half the main task. Adaptation to real-life scenarios is
now necessary and is achieved through discussions with the appropriate authorities. These
discussions and adaptations can restructure the cases in either significant or minor ways. The
critical part of this process is proof-testing to identify overlooked weak or missing links. Since
the process is iterative, the case sketches will experience multiple reviews before achieving their
final form.
After an appropriate amount of iterative steps has been made, the cases become ready for
the application of the FBIM manual. However, it is important to understand that the tactical
process of formation is not done. The usage of FBIM and other manuals can reveal additional,
previously unseen links that could slightly or significantly change the tactic.
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
43
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 4
Results & Discussion
This chapter outlines the usage and the results of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model, within the
Danish context, focusing on the development of tactics and intervention strategies, supported
by the FBIM manual, dataset, and validation interviews. Moreover, this chapter presents the
results of usage of the FBIM manual and discusses the elements that deviate from the manual.
Overall, the chapter’s objective is to illustrate the practical usage of the FBIM in Danish realities
based on two case studies.
4.1
Process for applying FBIM in a Danish context
This section focuses on the application of FBIM within a Danish context. It investigates the
practical understanding of FBIM through its application in two different cases, each with two
scenarios, which will be detailed below. By investigating these specific cases, this section aims
to demonstrate effective use and documentation of FBIM of realistic emergency scenarios within
the Danish context.
The scenarios provide a practical foundation for understanding how the model can be applied
across various fire scenarios. Moreover, the cases will be thoroughly investigated, and the
timelines will be collected in a Gantt chart, indicating the entire process of the intervention.
From the registration of the fire to the beginning of control and extinguishing of the fire. Lastly,
this section shows that FBIM can be applied in the Danish context, with some adjustments,
which will be discussed later in the project.
4.1.1
Case 1 - Scenario 1
The fire brigade intervention model is applied to case 1, scenario 1, concerning an EV-fire in
the parking cellar at the -2 level. In this scenario, the simultaneous intervention of two brigades
is considered. The tactical approach of the fire brigade is seen in figure 4.1 below.
The usage of the charts follows a logical sequence, established based on Danish Fire Intervention
protocol and interviews. This type of approach creates clarity and consistency in the execution
of tasks from registration of the fire to its extinguished.
For clarity and simplification, the documentation of the tactics and simulation will be done
by providing a sequential trajectory of chart usage on the left side of the page below, while
inserting a representation of 4.1 in the top right corner. The charts that are discussed on the
current page will be indicated in green color and the charts that the user already passed will be
marked with grey color.

As the hoses are now charged with water, search
and rescue can begin.
The search and rescue is
divided into two types of actions called primary
and secondary search.
Both searches are con-
ducted in this scenario and is displayed in figure 4.13.
The primary search will require Firefighter 2 (F2) to
walk with a thermal camera in all the rooms and
behind every corner of the fire compartment, while
Firefighter 1 (F1) will protect the F2 with the charged
hose.
The secondary search is performed in the vicinity of
the burning car and behind the elevator, by both F1 and F2. This
search requires crawling on the floor to check with a thermal camera
to be completely sure that there are no people that needs rescuing
from the compartment.
Model Input:

seconds.

As the search and rescue had a successful completion, SHT #3 is
ready to engage and support SHT #1 in the extinguishing of the
fire.
The time from the beginning of chart 1, till SHT #3 is ready to
support SHT #1 and the extinguishing process begins: 2448.32
seconds.
The electric vehicle has many components that can catch fire, in-
fluencing the burn time and potentially extending the extinguishing
process to up to 120 minutes or more, especially if a defensive
extinguishment tactic is used.
Everything depends on the damage, size, type of battery, etc.
Furthermore, the battery must cool down for 60 minutes before
the car can be hooked up to a trailer and transported away from
the cellar.

Table 4.1 presented above, contains a complete chronology of events, gathered during the
simulation of the fire brigade’s intervention. It includes important timing details necessary
for monitoring the sequence of events. The table contains the time of registration of the fire,
the fire brigade’s arrival, the relay of orders, the start of the intervention, etc. Furthermore,
times critical to firefighters are highlighted in the table with a “(+ 20 min)” notation. This
indicates that the smoke helmet team (SHT) is expected to exit the compartment 20 minutes
after being deployed in the compartment, wearing the breathing apparatus. In addition, certain
parts in this table are interesting to discuss. Several factors include travel and arrival times,
the duration to establish a water supply connection, the time firefighters need for search and
rescue operations, and the procedures for controlling and extinguishing the fire.

4.1.2
Case 1 - Scenario 2
As in the previous scenario, the application of the fire brigade intervention model (FBIM) is
used in a case where the fire combustion happened in the parking cellar at -2 level. In this
scenario, only one brigade will arrive at the fire scene. The tactical approach of the fire brigade
is seen in the figure 4.16 below.
Usage of the charts follows a logical sequence, which is established based on Danish Fire
Intervention protocol and interview. This type of approach creates clarity and consistency in
the execution of tasks from registration of the fire to its extinguishment.
The process of gathering information and creating a tactic is based on the same methodology
as in scenario 1, the chart will be described at each step, to understand the process and what
data were used to create this tactic.
As in scenario 1 for the users’ purposes, orientation in the tactic will be done by providing
a sequential trajectory of chart usage on the left side of the page below and inserting a
representation of figure 4.16 in the top right corner, which will give an overview of where the
tacit user is positioned. The charts that are discussed on the current page will be indicated in
green color and the charts that the user already passed will be marked with grey color.

The combustion of the electrical vehicle (EV) started
in the cellar at -2 level, which led to the spread
of smoke towards the ceiling. The temperature of
smoke at the ceiling causes the activation of sprinkler
systems in the fire compartment above the EV (3
taps). Depressurizing the sprinkler system causes the
verification system to start. After confirmation of
the depressurizing of the system, the system sends
an alarm further to the fire service.

4.1.3
Case 2 - Scenario 1
The fire brigade intervention model (FBIM) is applied when fire outbreaks happen in the car
park on the 6th floor. In the scenario, the simultaneous intervention of two brigades occurred.
The tactical approach of the fire brigade is seen in Figure 4.30 below.
The usage of the charts follows a logical sequence, which is established based on Danish Fire
Intervention protocol and interviews. This type of approach creates clarity and consistency in
the execution of tasks from registration of the fire to its extinguishment.
For user purposes, orientation in the tactics will be done by providing a sequential trajectory of
chart usage on the left side of the page below and inserting a representation of 4.30 in the top
right corner, which will give an overview of where the tacit user is positioned. The charts that
are discussed on the current page will be indicated in green color and the charts that the user
already passed will be marked with grey color.
Figure 4.30: Intervention tactic Case 2, Scenario 1
Firefighter Engineering - Use of Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
81
==PAGE:90==
The combustion of the electrical vehicle (EV) started
in the parking building on the 6th floor, which led
to the spread of smoke towards the ceiling. The
temperature of smoke at the ceiling causes the acti-
vation of sprinkler systems in the fire compartment
above the EV (2 taps). Depressurizing the sprinkler
system causes the verification system to start. After
confirmation of the depressurizing of the system, the
system sends an alarm further to the fire service.
Model Input:
Time to activate automatic detection or
suppression system: sprinkler system 45.00 seconds
Time to depressurize the system and activate the alarm: The
sprinkler system depressurizes in 50.00 seconds (> 2000m2)
[Table A (4), Figure 1]
Time delay for alarm verification: 20.00 seconds [Table B
(1)]
The time that is taken from the registration to send an alarm
further to fire services takes in a total of 115.00 seconds
At this step, the flowchart is split into two parallel trajectories:
Response 1 and Response 2. The Response 1 (R1) flow will be
examined first, to see what type of procedures the R1 needs to
complete before R1 is ready to arrive on the kurb side of the fire
scene. The same must be done with Response 2 (R2), which will
be subsequently done after the R1 investigation.
The dispatch process of firefighters went through the sprinkler
detection system to the ABA system, which sends the message
straight to the dispatch system.
The system does everything
automatically, so there will be no delay in the form of receiving and
giving the information further. This is based on the computer-aided
dispatch system (CAD system) that is installed. Besides that, the
T˚arnby Fire Station (TMFS) receives messages simultaneously with
the alarm central.
Model input:
Time to relay dispatch information: 0.00 seconds. [Table D
(2)]
The time that it takes to dispatch resources is 0.00 seconds.

Searching and rescue can begin after the water is charged in the
